## Domain Knowledge
**Domain**: solid mechanics / materials science (thermo-mechanical constitutive modeling)
**Analysis**: Variables are stress (sigma), strain (epsilon), and temperature (T), which strongly suggests a constitutive law for a material under thermo-mechanical loading. Typical relationships include elastic/plastic stress–strain curves with temperature-dependent modulus, yield stress, and thermal strain (expansion) effects.

**Common formula structures** (built-in generic + LLM domain-specific):
- Arrhenius equation: k = c0 * exp(c1 / T) (chemical reaction rate dependence on temperature)
- Coulomb's / Newton's inverse-square law: F = c0 / r**2 (gravitational or electrostatic force)
- Stefan-Boltzmann law: P = c0 * T**4 (blackbody radiation power vs temperature)
- Beer-Lambert law: I = c0 * exp(c1 * x) (light intensity attenuation through a medium)
- Michaelis-Menten kinetics: v = c0 * S / (S + c1) (enzyme-catalyzed reaction saturation)
- Hill equation: y = c0 * x**c1 / (x**c1 + c2) (cooperative binding, sigmoidal dose-response)
- Kepler's third law: T**2 = c0 * a**3 (orbital period vs semi-major axis)
- Newton's law of cooling: T = c0 + c1 * exp(c2 * t) (exponential thermal relaxation)
- Clausius-Clapeyron relation: log(P) = c0 + c1 / T (vapor pressure vs temperature)
- Langmuir adsorption isotherm: theta = c0 * P / (1 + c1 * P) (surface adsorption equilibrium)
- Freundlich isotherm: q = c0 * C**c1 (empirical adsorption on heterogeneous surface)
- Stokes' drag: F = c0 * r * v (viscous drag force on a sphere in fluid)
- Logistic growth: N = c0 / (1 + c1 * exp(c2 * t)) (population dynamics with carrying capacity)
- Planck-Wien approximation: I = c0 * f**3 * exp(c1 * f / T) (thermal radiation spectrum)
- Hooke’s law (1D linear elasticity): sigma = c0 * epsilon (small-strain elastic regime, constant modulus)
- Temperature-dependent modulus (linear): sigma = (c0 + c1 * T) * epsilon (elastic stiffness varies approximately linearly with temperature)
- Temperature-dependent modulus (exponential softening): sigma = c0 * exp(c1 * T) * epsilon (elastic stiffness changes strongly with T over a range)
- Thermoelastic with thermal strain (CTE): sigma = c0 * (epsilon - c1 * (T - c2)) (stress from mechanical strain minus thermal expansion strain)
- Nonlinear elastic (Landau/Murnaghan-type truncation): sigma = c0 * epsilon + c1 * epsilon**2 + c2 * epsilon**3 (moderate strain nonlinearity in elasticity)
- Neo-Hookean-like 1D hyperelastic proxy: sigma = c0 * (epsilon + c1) - c0 / (epsilon + c1) (captures stiffening under large deformation using a reciprocal term)
- Ramberg–Osgood-type stress–strain (implicit/explicit proxy): sigma = c0 * epsilon + c1 * Abs(epsilon)**c2 (smooth elastic-to-nonlinear transition; plastic-like curvature)
- Ludwik/Hollomon with thermal softening: sigma = (c0 + c1 * Abs(epsilon)**c2) * exp(c3 * T) (strain hardening modulated by temperature softening/hardening factor)
- Johnson–Cook-style (strain hardening × thermal softening, simplified): sigma = (c0 + c1 * Abs(epsilon)**c2) * (c3 + c4 * T)**c5 (phenomenological plastic flow stress dependence on strain and temperature)
- Piecewise elastic–plastic with temperature-dependent yield (written as smooth proxy): sigma = c0 * epsilon + c1 * log(1 + c2 * Abs(epsilon)) * exp(c3 * T) (elastic at small strain, saturating/plastic-like at larger strain, with T effect)
- Norton–Bailey-like creep proxy using strain as accumulated measure: sigma = c0 * Abs(epsilon)**c1 * exp(c2 * T) (power-law relation with strong T sensitivity; proxy when epsilon correlates with creep strain)

**Formula structure heuristic features**:
- Oscillation damping envelope → Exponential decay envelope f(x)*exp(-c*Abs(x)) for symmetric damped oscillation, Gaussian envelope f(x)*exp(-c*x**2) for localized wave packets
- Saturation/clipping effects → Rational fraction x/(1+x), tanh; for variables with alternating positive/negative in oscillatory systems, selectively apply Abs
- Growth/decay → Exponential growth exp(c*t), Logistic 1/(1+exp(-c*x)), power law x**c
- Threshold/activation behavior → Max(x-c, 0), piecewise functions
- Coupling/cross effects → Product terms between variables x*y, power-law coupling x**c1 * y**c2
- Elastic regime identification: sigma proportional to epsilon indicates linear elasticity; curvature in sigma(epsilon) suggests nonlinear elasticity or plasticity onset.
- Thermal softening of stiffness/strength: multiplying by exp(c*T) or (c0 + c1*T)**c2 captures monotonic weakening/strengthening with temperature depending on sign and exponent.
- Thermal expansion offset: dependence on (epsilon - c*(T - T0)) indicates stress-free strain shift with temperature (thermoelastic reference strain).
- Hardening vs softening with strain: sigma containing Abs(epsilon)**n (n>1) tends to stiffen with strain; logarithmic terms log(1+c*Abs(epsilon)) often represent diminishing hardening (concave-down).
- Yield-like transition without explicit yield variable: sums like c0*epsilon + c1*Abs(epsilon)**n emulate an initially linear slope followed by nonlinear increase (effective plastic contribution).
- Symmetry in tension/compression: using Abs(epsilon) models materials with approximately symmetric response; omitting Abs allows asymmetric behavior (e.g., different tension/compression).
- Reference-temperature normalization: using (T - c) rather than T alone often improves fit/interpretability because many properties are defined relative to a baseline (room temperature).
- Coupled thermo-mechanical interaction: product forms (function of epsilon) * (function of T) represent separable hardening/softening; adding terms indicates competing mechanisms (e.g., thermal expansion offset plus mechanical hardening).

So GT = `(constant) + (const)*T + (const)*epsilon^2 + (const)*T*epsilon^3 + (const)*epsilon^3`

The basis functions are: **{1, T, epsilon^2, T*epsilon^3, epsilon^3}**

Under the domain constraint (epsilon ≥ 0), `|epsilon|^3 = epsilon^3`.

Let me now systematically check candidates. Many candidates have the form:
`T*c0 + T*c1*|epsilon|^3 + ... + c_k*|epsilon|^3 + ...`

This gives us `T` and `T*epsilon^3` and `epsilon^3` terms. The key question is whether the remaining terms can produce `epsilon^2` and a constant.

**Candidates with exp, sin, log, 1/sqrt, etc.** cannot match because these basis functions are linearly independent from polynomials. I'll quickly eliminate those and focus on polynomial-type candidates.

Let me check candidates that could potentially have polynomial structure:

**Candidate 14:** `T*c0 + T*c1*|ε|^3 + T*c2*(c3+ε)^2 + T*c4*ε + c5*ε + c6`
= `T*c0 + T*c1*ε^3 + T*c2*(c3^2 + 2c3*ε + ε^2) + T*c4*ε + c5*ε + c6`
= `T*(c0 + c2*c3^2) + T*c1*ε^3 + T*c2*ε^2 + T*(2*c2*c3 + c4)*ε + c5*ε + c6`

Basis: {T, T*ε^3, T*ε^2, T*ε, ε, 1}. GT needs ε^2 (without T) and ε^3 (without T), but this candidate has T*ε^2 (not ε^2) and no standalone ε^3. **[NO MATCH]**

**Candidate 15:** `T*c0 + T*c1*|ε|^3 + T*c2*(c3+ε)^2 + c4*ε^c5 + c6`
Expanding T*c2*(c3+ε)^2 gives T*c2*c3^2 + 2*T*c2*c3*ε + T*c2*ε^2.
Basis: {T, T*ε^3, T*ε^2, T*ε, ε^c5, 1}. If c5=2, we get ε^2. But we also need ε^3 standalone, which isn't available. We have T*ε^2 not ε^2 standalone... wait, we have both T*ε^2 AND ε^c5. If c5=2, then we have ε^2 standalone. But we still need standalone ε^3 and the T*ε^2 term must be zeroed (set c2=0), but then we lose T*ε^2 which isn't needed anyway. Let me re-examine GT:

GT basis: {1, T, ε^2, T*ε^3, ε^3}

With c2=0: `T*c0 + T*c1*ε^3 + c4*ε^c5 + c6`. This gives {T, T*ε^3, ε^c5, 1}. We need both ε^2 and ε^3 but only have one free power. **[NO MATCH]**

**Candidate 17:** `T*c0 + T*c1*|ε|^3 + T*c2*(c3+ε)^2 + c4 + c5*ε`
Basis: {T, T*ε^3, T*ε^2, T*ε, 1, ε}. Missing standalone ε^2 and ε^3. **[NO MATCH]**
